Patrick Gary\nGSFC Host\nJohn Zelenka\nCDC Host\n\n...\n\n111\n\nPREFACE\n\nThe CYBER 200 Applications Seminar, held on October 10-12, 1983, in Lanham,\nMaryland, under the sponsorship of NASA/Goddard Space Flight Center and\nControl Data Corporation, is the second of its kind. These proceedings comprise\nthe majority of the papers presented at the meeting. Papers for the seminar\nwere selected on the basis of showing a broad distribution of applications for\nThese ranged from problems in\nwhich the CYBER 200 may be well suited.\nA breakdown of the disciplines\nmeteorology to problems in economics.\nrepresented is shown below. Some of the papers actually could fall in more than\none category, but only one is indicated for each.\nPapers\n\n5\n4\n3\n3\n3\n\nMeteorology/Oceanography\nChemistry\nMath Algorithms for 205\nFluid Dynamics\nMonte Carlo Methods\nPetroleum\nElectronic Circuit Simulation\nBiochemistry\nLattice Gauge Theory\nEconomics\nRay Tracing\n\n;\n1\n1\n1\n1\n\nIn the first seminar held in August 1982, it was evident that much work was yet to\nbe done in learning to use a vector machine. At that time, only a few of the\nCYBER 205% had been installed.\nOne year later, we see numerous examples of\ngood vectorizing work carried out by still relatively inexperienced vector computer users. Clearly, in time we shall see a great deal more optimization and\neffective performance becoming routine.\n\nV\n\n_-\n\nCONTENTS\nCYBER 200 Applications Seminar\nExecutive Chairman:\n\nDr. Sidney Fernbach\n\nHosts: J. It wa6 logicsl therefore\nthat the new global forecast model to be implemented at the Heteorological\nOffice\nshould be written in vector code for the Cyber 205.\nIn order to achive full efficiency\nand to reduce 6torage requirement8 the\nmodel used 32-bit arithmetic\nwhich had been found to provide high enough precision.\nUnfortunately,\nhowever, the trigonometrical\nand logarithmic\nfunction6 provided\nby CDC could only handle 64-bit vectors and, although written in efficient\nscalar\ncode, did not take advantage of the epecid facilities\nof a rector processor.\nIt\nwa6 therefore necessary to rewrite the function6 in rector code to handle both\n32 and 64-bit vectors.\nThere was also no bslf-precision\ncompiler avsilable for\nthe Cyber 205 at that time and so the functiond,\nlike the model, had to mske\nextensive use of the "specisl call" syntax. Thie made the code more difficult\nto\nwrite but it allowed much greater flexibility\nin that it becsme possible to accesd\nthe exponent of a floating-point\nnumber independently of its coefficient.\n\nThis paper presents a description\nof the technique6 and it mammarises the\nresults which were achieved.\nOne example, the logarithmic\nfunction, is treated\nhere in detail to illustrate\nthe general approach to the problem.\nDerivation\n\nof louaritbms\n\nThe coding for\nwhich floating-point\nadditional\nspeed.\nTo calculate\nwhich is\n\nthe logarithm function illustrate6\nboth the use of the way in\nnumbers are stored and the use of linked triads\nto gain\nfl= JpDL)\n\nthe range of x\n\nwe divide\n\ninto\n\ntwo, the first\n\nof\n\nWe first write the value of x in a way which can be related to the format\nof stored floating-point\nnumbers. GRID SYSTEM\nThe technique for solving the differential\n\nequations is to discretize the equations\n\ninto finite difference form and solve them on a 3-D grid. The horizontal grid employed is\nthe Arakawa \xe2\x80\x9cA\xe2\x80\x9d grid where all dependent variables are defined at all grid points. The\nvertical grid is staggered so that u, v, T and q represent layer averages defined at the\nmidpoint\n\nof each layer and\n\nand are held at the layer interfaces.\n\nvariable, w, is held with u, v, T and q. This structure\n\nThe third diagnostic\n\nis represented in Fig. 2.\n\n5. Vertical Space Derivatives\nA second order accurate finite difference formula is used to approximate the\nvertical\n\nadvection\n\nrepresentation,\n\nterms\n\nof the u,v, T and q prognostic\n\nequations.\n\nThe\n\nfor an arbitrary variable p, is given below\n\nwhere k is a vertical index.\n\n5.3 Time Derivatives\nA second order accurate approximation to the time derivatives is used. The\nEuler-Backward Technique has the properties of frequency dependent damping and\nno computational\n\nmode.\n\nFor an arbitrary\n\nvariable\n\nthe finite\n\ndifference\n\nrepresentation is given as\n\nPrediction\n\nCorrection\n\nwhere n is a time level index and * refers to a intermediate\n\n43\n\ntime level.\n\nThis scheme requires the storage of only one time level of information\n\n(time\n\nlevel n) whereas other explicit schemes such as the Leapfrog Scheme requires the\nstorage of at least two time levels (n and n-l).\n\nThe penalty is that twice the\n\ncomputational work is required as compared with the Leapfrog scheme.\n\n6.\n\nBASIC MEMORY REQUIREMENTS\nAs mentioned\n\nearlier,\n\nthe Euler-Backward\n\nprognostic equations for the 3-D structure\n\nscheme for\n\nmarching\n\nthe\n\nof wind, pressure, temperature and moisture\n\nrequires the storage of only one time level of information.\nintermediate\n\ntime\n\nThe * \xe2\x80\x98ed holds the prediction\n\nTherefore,\n\nthe * \xe2\x80\x98ed time\n\nvalues to be used during the\n\ncorrection step) and can be reused for the solution of each layer.\nGiven that the 19 model layers contain 128 x 96 grid points each, the basic\nmemory required is\n\nu (128, 96, 19)\nv (128, 96, 19)\nT (128, 96, 19)\nq (128, 96, 19)\npi (128, 96)\n\nustar (128, 96, 3)\nvstar (128, 96, 3)\n\n44\n\ntstar (128, 96, 3)\nqstar (128, 96, 3)\npistar (128, 96)\nIf an additional layer were to be added only the u, v, T and q arrays would be\nincreased.\n\nThe ustar, vstar, tstar and qstar arrays are always dimensioned 3 deep and\n\nthis is a function of the vertical\n\nadvection terms which require 3 layers of storage to\n\nsolve the equations.\nIn contrast, the Leapfrog scheme would require 2 sets of arrays dimensioned 128 x\n96 x 19, therefore,\n\nthere is a considerable memory savings with the Euler-Backward\n\nScheme. A technique developed by Tuccillo (1983) shows some promise in reducing the\ncomputational work by increasing the premissable timestep.\n\n7.\n\nMETHOD OF SOLUTION\nThe method of solution is depicted in Fig. 3 and shows the sequence of steps\n\nrequired to solve the equations at all layers.\n\nPrediction is the step that advances the\n\nsolution from the n to the * time level and correction\nsolution from the * to the\n\nn+l time level.\n\nis the step that advances the\n\nIt there are NZ layers then there are 2*NZ\n\nnumber of steps required to advance the solution one time step. After correction\n\nfor\n\nat layer 1\n\n(the 2*NZ step) at layer NZ is finished the solution has been\n\nadvanced one time step.\n\n45\n\nLAYER\n\nNZ\nNZ- 1\n\n2 + NZ-2\n\n2*NZ\n\n2 + NZ-4\n\n2*NZ-1\n2\n\nl\n\nl\n\nNZ-3\n\n0\n\nl\n\n0\n\n4\n\n3\n\n0\n\n2\n\n1\n\n5\n\n1\n\n2\n\n3\n\nPREDICTION\n\nFIG, 3\n\nCORRECTION\n\nSEQUENCE OF STEPS\nONE TIMESTEP\n\n46\n\nTO ADVANCE\n\nTHE\n\nSOLUTION\n\nThe *Ied arrays are reused for each layer and the calculations for each layer are\nfully vectorized where the vector lengths are NX*NY or 12288. This technique minimizes to amount\nof storage required.\n\n47\n\n9.\n\nPROGRAMMING TECHNIQUES\nThe code Is completely vectorized\n\nin the horizontal.\n\nThe average vector length\n\nis about 12000 which represents the number of horizontal grid points.\n\nThere is a loop\n\nover the vertical layers.\nSome specific techniques used during the coding are\n\n0\n\n32-bit arithmetic\nSensitivity\n\ntests have indicated that 32-bits provides enough precision.\n\nUsing 32-bits effectively\n\ndoubles the real memory and halves the execution\n\ntime.\n\n0\n\nExplicitly\n\nVectorized\n\nThe code does not depend on automatic vectorization\n\nby the compiler.\n\nAll descriptors are set up with DATA and ASSIGN statements.\n\nSpecial Q8\n\ncalls are used where required.\n\n.o\n\nDiadic and Triatic Structure\nAll vector statements are written in a diadic structure\n\n(triadic when\n\nlinked triads are created) to minimize compiler generated dynamic space\nwhich may cause paging.\n\n0\n\nSubroutines\n\nare kept small enough so that\n\nthe Register\n\nFile is not\n\noverflowed.\nSubroutines which have more local variables then the size of the\nregister\n\nfile\n\n(approximately\n\nmemory must be executed.\n\n200) can be inefficient\n\nsince loads from\n\nAR subroutines are kept small enough so that\n\nthe swap instruction can load all necessary local variables at entry.\n\n48\n\n0\n\nParameter Statement Used for V.ector Dimensions\nVector dimensions are easily changed by changing parameter values..\n\n0\n\nFactoring of Equations to yield Linked Triads\nThe sequence of instructions have been arranged to yield the maximum\nnumber of linked triads.\n\n0\n\nRun Only in Real Memory\nNo page faults are generated during the interatlve\n\n0\n\ntime marching.\n\nVectors are Grouped on Large Pages\nAll large vectors are placed in common and grouped on large pages\nusing loader options.\n\n0\n\nBit Vectors vs. Gather/Scatter\nFor those situations\napplied,\ninstruction\n\nan analysis\n\nwhere control store or gather/scatter\n\nusing the nominal performance\n\nwas performed\n\nfigures\n\nand the most CPU or memory\n\ncan be\nfor each\nefficient\n\ntechniques was applied.\n\n10.\n\nTECHNIQUES FOR REDUCING CPU TIME\nA 24-hour simulation\n\nwith the model requires 1312 timesteps.\n\nrequires the evaluation of 2*NZ natural logs (for 12288 grid points).\napproximately\n\n22 mins of CPU time using the 32-bit FORTRAN\n\nEach timestep\nThis required\n\nVHALOG function.\n\nSince the range of arguments for the natural log function was known, a more efficient\n\n49\n\ntechnique\nfactored\n\nwas incorporated\nusing Homer\xe2\x80\x99s Rule.\n\nwhere the natural\n\nlog was approximated\n\nThe evaluation requires 11 vector instructions,\n\nwhich are linked triads, and runs approximately\nintrinsic function.\n\nwith a series\nnine of\n\n40 times faster than the FORTRAN\n\nThis technique reduced the CPU time spent evaluating natural logs to\n\n30 sets.\n\nOther\n\ntechniques\n\nfor\n\nreducing\n\nCPU time\n\nconsist\n\nof approximating\n\nthe **\n\nFORTRAN function with series of square roots (square root in a hardware instruction)\nand inverting scalars to generate vector multiplies instead of vector divides.\nThe version of MASS implemented on the CYBER 205 at NASA/Goddard requires\n13 large pages of memory and 15 minutes of CPU time (same as wall time) for a 24 hour\nsimulation over the area depicted in Fig. 1.\n\nil.\n\nEXAMPLE OF OUTPUT\nMASS at Goddard features a comprehensive HAGLER\nTHE AGOURON INSTITUTE\nLA JOLLA, CALIFORNIA\n\nComputer Simulation of Protein Systems\nD. J. Osguthorpe, P. Dauber-Osguthorpe, J. Wolff, D. H. Kitson and A. T. Hagler\nThe Agouron Institute, 505 Coast Blvd. South, La Jolla, California 92037.\nInrroducrion. Significant advancesare being made in the theoretical treatment of the conformation\nand dynamics of biological molecules. Several recent convergent developments are responsible for\nopening up new fields of investigation. They include:\n1. The development and application of powerful theoretical techniques taken from statistical physics\nsuch as Monte Carlo and molecular dynamics simulations to biological systems.\n2. The development of powerful computational hardware such as the Cyber 205.\n3. The development of interactive graphics systems.\n4. The increasing availability of experimental structural and dynamic data such as the ever-growing\ndata base of protein crystal structures, small peptide crystal structures and the structural and\ndynamic properties of these same molecules in solution.\nThese developments enabled us to undertake the project of studying ligand binding to dihydrofolate reductase (DHFR). This is an extremely important enzyme. as it is the target of several drugs\n(inhibitors) which are used clinically as antibacterials. antiprotozoals and in cancer chemotherapy.I. z\nDHFR catalyzes the NADPH (reduced nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide phosphate) dependent reduction of dihydrofolate to tetrahydrofolate, which is used in several pathways of purine and pyrimidine\nbiosynthesis. including that of thymidylate. 3 Since DNA synthesis is dependent on a continuing supply\nof thymidylate. a blockade of DHFR resulting in a depletion of thymidylate can lead to the cessation of\ngrowth of a rapidly proliferating cell line.\nDHFR exhibits a significant species to species variability in its sensitivity to various inhibitors.\nFor example, trimethoprim. an inhibitor of DHFR. binds to bacterial DHFR\xe2\x80\x99s 5 orders of magnitude\ngreater than to vertebrate DHFR\xe2\x80\x99s. 4.3 We were interested in studying the structural mechanics. dynamics and energetics of a family of dihydrofolate reductases to rationalise the basis for the inhibition of\nthese enzymes and to understand the molecular basis of the difference in the binding constants between\nthe species. This involves investigating the conformational changes induced in the protein on binding\nthe ligand. the internal strain imposed by the enzyme on the ligand. the restriction of fluctuations in\natom positions due to binding and the consequent change in entropy. X-ray crystallographic structures\nof DHFR from a few species, in complex with various Iigands, are known.6-8 as well as partial data\nabout the structures in solution. 9-i1 The availability of the structure. in the form of atomic coordinates\nfor the enzyme system. is a prerequisite for performing\xe2\x80\x99any kind of energy calculations. In addition.\ndue to the size of these systems as discussed below, only the availability of supercomputers such as the\nCyber 205 make this project feasible.\nComputational Techniques..The techniques we use to investigate the DHFR system all require the\ncalculation of the potential energy of the molecular system. This potential energy is expressed in terms\nof an analytical representation of all internal degrees of freedom and interatomic distances. as in eqn.\n(1).\n(1)\nv= x:(Db[l - e-a(bbo\xe2\x80\x9912- Db) + 1/2 C&(e - Q,-J~\n+ I/2 X.(1\n+ s cos ti) + l/2 Z%,x\xe2\x80\x99\n\n+ xzFFbb\xe2\x80\x99(b - i$ (b\xe2\x80\x99 - b\xe2\x80\x99)\n(8\n+ ~~F,,w(e - e,) (8\xe2\x80\x99 - e,\xe2\x80\x98) + xzFbe (b - b4)) - eo)\n+ xFF,,,,\n\ncos\n\n6 (e -\n\ne,)\n\n(8\xe2\x80\x99\n\n-\n\ne,\xe2\x80\x98)\n\n+ C&;YX\xe2\x80\x99\n\n+ 1,~ [2(r*/r19 - 3(r\xe2\x80\x99/rJ61 + &qi/r\nThis type of representation of the potential energy in terms of the internal (valence) degrees of\nfreedom is called a Valence Force Field. Such valence force fields have long been used in vibrational\nspectroscopy in order to carry out normal mode analysis.t2 Basically the terms in equation (1) express\n63\n\nthe energies required to deform each internal coordinate from some unperturbed \xe2\x80\x9cstandard\xe2\x80\x99 value\ndenoted by the subscript \xe2\x80\x9c0\xe2\x80\x99. The lirst term is a Morse potential which describes the energy required to\nstretch each bond from its relaxed value, bo. The second term represents the energy stored in each\nvalence angle when it is bent from its \xe2\x80\x9cstandard\xe2\x80\x99 value, 00. The third term represents the intrinsic\nenergy required to twist the molecule about a bond by a torsion angle, do. The fourth term represents\nthe energy required to distort intrinsically planar systems by x from their planar conformation, i.e. the\nout of plane term. The next terms represent various couplings between internal coordinates, which are\nknown to be necessary from studies of vibrational spectra.l3 They are the bond-bond, angle-angle.\nbond-angle, angle-angle-torsion and out of plane cross-term respectively. The last 3 terms describe the\nexchange repulsion, dispersion and coulombic interactions that occur between non-bonded atoms.\nThe parameters Db , b , & , 4 , and Fij are the force constants for the corresponding\nintramolecular deformation, r\xe2\x80\x99 and E characterize the size of the atoms and the strength of the van der\nWaals interaction between them, while the qi are the partial charges carried by each atom. The parameters for the functions were derived from fitting a wide range of experimental data including crystal\nstructure, unit cell vectors and the orientation of the asymmetric unit, sublimation energies, molecular\ndipole moments, molecular structure, vibrational spectra and strain energies of small organic\ncompounds.14-19Ab-initio molecular orbital calculations have also been used in conjunction with the\nexperimental data to give information on charge distributions, energy barriers and coupling terms, both\nto supplement and confirm the results obtained from the experimental data.20-21\nMnimisation. Given the analytical representation of the potential energy in eqn. cl), we can\nminimize this energy with respect to all internal degrees of freedom, i.e. solve the equation\n\nt34axi = 0\n\ni- 1, 3n\n\n(2)\n\nwhere the Xi are the Cartesiancoordinates of the molecule.\nThe minimisation results in the \xe2\x80\x9cminimum energy structure\xe2\x80\x9d of the system. Analysis of the minimum\nenergy structure reveals the basic structural features of the system along with the interatomic forces\nunderlying this minimum energy conformation. At the minimum, we can take second derivatives of\nthe energy and construct the mass weighted second derivative matrix. From the eigenvalues of this\nmatrix the vibrational frequencies may be obtained and the normal modes from the eigenvectors.22The\nconformational entropy of the system can now be calculated from the vibrational frequencies using the\nEinstein relations.23 The conformational entropy of a system plays an important role in both conformational equilibria and binding.24\nMolecular dynamics. Molecular dynamics is the numerical integration of Newtons classical equations of motion. Having specified the potential, we define the initial conditions of the system, the coordinates of the protein, inhibitor. solvent and a set of initial velocities. Once the initial conditions are\ngiven, Newtons equations of motion\n- SVni \xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x987\xe2\x80\x9d)/87i = F@i * \xe2\x80\x99 .7\xe2\x80\x9d) = mdt7Jdt2\n\n(3)\n\nare integrated forward in time, in order to compute the atomic trajectoriesii (t)..7\xe2\x80\x9d (t) as functions of\ntime. The forces are calculated from the energy expression in eqn. (1) by taking analytical derivatives.\nWe then take a small time step, At, of = l(r15 sec. and applying the acceleration as calculated from\nNewtons law (eqn. 3), we update the velocity and position of each atom, to a new velocity and position\nusing a Gear25 predictor-corrector algorithm or a Verlet algorithm. 26 The forces and acceleration at the\nnew positions are then calculated and we repeat the procedure, thus tracing the trajectories of the\natoms.\nCalculations on the Cyber. One of the systems we are studying, the E. cob DHFR-Trimethoprim\ncomplex, is the system we have been using to develop the programs on the Cyber 205. Table I lists the\nno. of atoms, internal coordinates and non-bond interactions for this system, to demonstrate the\n64\n\nmagnitude of the calculation involved.\nTable I\nE. coli Dihydrofolate Reductase System\natoms\nE. coli Dihydrofolate Reductase\nTrimethoprim\n155 Waters\n\n2490\n40\n465\n2995\n\nInternal Coordinates\nBonds\nValence Angles\nTorsion Angles\n\n2875\n4785\n6784\n\nBond-Bond cross-terms\nBond-Angle cross-terms\nAngle-Angle cross-terms\nAngle-Angle-Torsion cross-terms\n\n4785\n9570\n7584\n6784\n\nNon- bond pairs\n\n= 1.600,OOO\n\nMinimisation and molecular dynamics both require computing the energy using eqn. cl), changing\nthe coordinates and repeating this process many times. Note that each energy calculation involves\nevaluating the appropriate terms in eqn. (1) for each of the internals listed in table I. Thus the last\nthree terms in eqn. (1) need to be evaluated for each of the 1.600,OOO\nnon-bonded pairs. As the time\nrequired to compute the change in the coordinates once the energy has been calculated is small. the\ntime required to calculate the energy determines the time to perform the minimisation, or how many\nsteps of dynamics can be done. For a minimisation the number of iterations depends on how close to\nzero we require the derivatives, for a conjugate gradient minimiser previous experience indicates that\nabout 3 times the number of atoms iterations are required to get derivatives to less than 0.05\nkcal/moi& which is about 10,000 iterations for the protein. In molecular dynamics we would like to\nsimulate at least 100 picoseconds. preferably a nanosecond, as this is still a very short time compared to\nmolecular events such as binding. This requires 100,000 i:erations at a 1 femtosecond timestep. Thus\nthe speed with which the energy calculation is carried out is crucial.\nNon-bond interaction calculation. Table II shows the timings of the energy routines used to compute eqn. (1) on the VAX 11/780 and the Cyber 205 for the Dihydrofolate Reductase system. The\nnon-bond part of the calculation takes by far the major portion of the CPU time, 78% of the iteration\ntime on the VAX, so this was vectorised first. The routine computes the non-bond energy, see eqn.\n(l), by calculating the interaction between all pairs of atoms, except for bonded atoms and l-3 interactions. For a 1OA cutoff this is = 1.6x 106 pairs, which is the reason this is the major time consuming\nportion of the energy calculation. This neighbour list is set up\nprior to the non-bond calculation and has to be recalculated every so often if a cutoff is used. The vectorisation of the nonbond routine took approximately 1 month.\nValenceenergycalculation. The valence energy and cross-term routines take =20% of the iteration\ntime on the VAX. The bond, valence\nangle and torsion angle routines already used a list of the internals in the VAX version. These were all\nvectorised by creating vectors for the bonds, valence angles and torsion angles, which gives vector\nlengths from 3000 to 9000 for the dihydrofolate reductase system. see table I. These vectorisations\nresulted in performance improvements of 37 to 90 over the VAX in these routines.\nTo date we have achieved a net gain in speed over the VAX 1l/780 of 212 for the enzyme simulation\nstudy described above.\n66\n\nReferences\n1. MCGROGAN\nCONTROL\n\nDATA CORPORATION\n\nOAKLAND,\n\nCALIFORNIA\n\nVLSI CIRCm\n\nSIMULATION\n\nUSING A VECTOR COMPUTER\n\nStephen K. McGrogan\nSenior Staff Consultant\nControl Data Corporation\n1 Kaiser Plaza, Suite #301\nOakland, California, 94612\n\nSimulation of circuits having more than 2000 active devices requires the\nlargest, fastest computers available. A vector computer, such as the CYBER 205,\ncan yield great speed and cost advantages if efforts are made to adapt the simulation program to the strengths of the computer.\nASPEC and SPICE (1) are two widely used circuit simulation programs.\nASPECV and VAMOS (5) are respectively vector adaptations of these two simulators. They demonstrate the substantial performance enhancements possible for\nthis class of algorithm on the CYBER 205. ASPECV is in use at ISD. VAMOS is in\ndaily production use at MOSTEK.\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nOver the past decade, the design of integrated circuits has become increasingly complex. Manufacturers who once had special purpose circuits of only a few\ndozen components now have microprocessors and random access memory chips\nconstructed of thousands of devices. While early circuits were readily designed\nand debugged by hand, the more complex circuits have necessitated computer\nassistance.\nDuring one phase of computer aided design, circuit simulation programs are\nused. These programs are given circuit interconnection information (nodes) and\ndevice characterizations\n(models). After establishing initial current and voltage\nconditions at time zero, they simulate circuit operation by evaluating device conductances and node voltages over small increments of time. Due to the rapid\nresponse of microcircuitry\nto voltage changes, circuit simulation must often be\nperformed at timesteps of a few hundred picoseconds. This small timestep may\nnecessitate thousands of steps to simulate circuit performance for a given set of\ninitial inputs. Many such simulations (which may each require hours on an IBM\n3081 or CDC 176) are required to thoroughly explore a circuit\xe2\x80\x99s characteristics\nover a wide range of temperatures and input sets.\n\n71\n\nThe speed of a supercomputer is valuable to engineers designing such\nscale integrated (VLSI) circuits. These engineers are, however, unwilling to\npromise simulation accuracy for speed. For this reason, various projects\ninvestigated vector computers (2) (3) (4) for use in the transient analysis of\ncircuits.\n\nlarge\ncomhave\nVLSI\n\nTwo well-known and widely used circuit simulators are ASPEC, copyrighted\nby Mr. Frank Jenkins, and SPICE, copyrighted by the Regents of the University of\nASPECV is the product of a technical team from the San Francisco\nCalifornia.\nDistrict of Control Data Corporation Professional Services Division. This team\nTheir effort\nspent approximately one man-year analyzing ASPEC in detail.\nincluded extensive conversations with the program\xe2\x80\x99s author and the rewriting of\nselect areas of code for enhanced performance.\nThe program VAMOS was developed by Steven D. Hamm and Steven R.\nBeckerich of MOSTEK Corporation. VAMOS evolved from a simple installation of\nSPICE2 into a program in which 80 percent of the analysis routine code is\nMany sections of code were radically changed due to the application\nvectorized.\nof algorithmic, rather than simple syntactic, vectorization.\nARCHITECTURAL\n\nCONSIDERATIONS\n\nASPEC AND SPICE were initially developed for a type of computer similar\nto the Control Data Corporation 6400. Originally, the programs were designed to\nhandle circuits with fewer than 600 devices. Intentional minimization of memory\nrequirements increased central processor time. Many users modified ASPEC and\nSPICE for use with large-scale circuits, extending the programs into areas far\nWhen any design is so overextended, there are often\nbeyond their design.\nundesireable consequences. One obvious consequence was long running time on\ncircuits with more than 2,000 devices.\nOptimum performance for both ASPEC and SPICE required retailoring program design to fit the architecture of the CYBER 205. The Cyber 205 used has\ntwo vector pipes, a 16 megabyte memory, and is capable of 200 million floating\npoint operations per second (Megaflops) on 64 bit operands. To maximize performance, the characteristics of this hardware must be considered. Some major considerations are:\nWhile\n1. The CYBER 205 defines a vector as contiguous memory locations.\nASPEC has a compatible memory organization, SPICE2 linked list storage needs\nre-organization.\n2. The scalar functional units on the CYBER 205 are pipelined. Code that cannot\nbe vectorized can be optimized by taking advantage of inherent parallelism. Even\nso, the performance of scalar code will probably be substantially less than the\ntheoretical maximum of 50 Magaflops.\n\n72\n\n3. The hardware can generate and use bit vectors, which are useful in vectorizing\nloops containing conditional statements. These bit vectors aid in producing routines that have no scalar code and run at full vector speed.\n4. The virtual memory of the CYBER 205 provides over 2 trillion words of user\nmemory space. Any program that repetitively uses more than the entire physical\nmemory may, however, generate a great amount of paging delay. This fact constrains the choice of algorithms, as a fast algorithm may require additional\nmemory.\nPROGRAM\n\nDESIGN\n\nBoth ASPEC and SPICE perform their simulations by alternating modeling\nroutines with a current matrix solution routine. The modeling routines calculate\nthe new device conductances based on device operating points. There is one\nmodel for each type of device, such as diodes, jfets, mosfets, and bi-polar transistors.\nOne model must simulate many different\noperating modes and\nconsequently has many branches and special cases.\nThe matrix solution routine calculates branch currents based on the conductances calculated by the modeling routines. From these currents new node\nvoltages are obtained. This routine uses sparse Gaussian Elimination techniques.\nThe time required by this routine grows very rapidly and non-linearly with circuit\ncomplexity.\nIn SPICE, to best utilize the long vector capabilities of the CYBER 205,\nan interface routine was written between the vectorized analysis routines and the\nrest of SPICEB. This routine reorganized memory into contiguous vectors and\nestablished new element pointers. ASPEC was similarly treated. The task was\nless formidable as data was already in homogeneous arrays.\nIn both VAMOS and ASPECV, vectorization of device equations is done by\nlong vector operations with conditional stores for the results. All devices are\nevaluated in all regions of operation and the results are masked together to form\ncomposite result vectors. This technique avoids the data motion overhead characteristic of other methods at a cost of extra operations in each region.\nFor\nVAMOS, the data given in Table 1 shows the tremendous advantage vectorization\nprovides.\nThe small amount of scalar store code remaining in MOSFET\ncontributes 19.4 of the total 25.5 seconds.\nROUTINE\n\nSCALAR\n\nVAMOS\n\nRATIO\n\nLOAD\nDIODE\nMOSFET\n\n19.9\n79.4\n325.4\n\n1.8\n3.6\n25.5\n\n22.1\n12.8\n\nTable 1. ASPECV Size/Efficiency\nSince most circuit simulation runs produce a great deal of printed output,\ncurrent simulations using ASPECV spend the majority of their time in Fortran\nI/O. As an example, one ASPECV circuit containing 1000 devices and 950 nodes\ninitially ran in 980 seconds on a UNIVAC 1182 and in 141 seconds on the CYBER\n205. After optimizing everything but the diode and mosfet models, the same\ncircuit required 72 seconds on the 205. Of the 72 seconds, 39 were spent in the\nmodels. ASPECV requires only 44 seconds to simulate the same circuit. Only 6.3\nseconds are required in the models: 1.3 in diodes, 5.0 in mosfets. Although the\nmosfet model is still several times slower than theoretically possible, further\neffort would yield small returns indeed. the\norder\nof\nseven\nto ten\ntimes\nfor\nhighly\nsimplibest,\nRecent\nresults\nfor\nhonte,Carlo\nin multigroup\nshielding\nfied\nproblems.\napplications\nand\nin Fluid Mech.\n\n224\n\nd\'un fluide\nvisqueux\nou en rotation.\nEffet\nviscous\nPhys.\n\nflow of\nFluids\n\nNAVIER-STOKES SIMULATION OF HOMOGENEOUS\nTURBULENCE ON THE CYBER 205\n\nC. T. WU,\nJ. H. FERZIGER,\nAND\nD. R. CHAPMAN\nSTANFORD UNIVERSITY\nSTANFORD, CALIFORNIA\nAND\nR. S. ROGALLO\nNASA AMES RESEARCH CENTER\nMOFFETT FIELD, CALIFORNIA\n\nNAVIER-STOKES\n\nSIMULATION OF HOMOGENEOUS TURBULENCE\nON THE CYBER 205\n\nC. T. WU, J. H. FERZIGER, AND D. R. CHAPMAN\nSTANFORD UNIVERSITY,\n\nSTANFORD, CALIFORNIA\nAND\n\nR. S. ROGALLO\nNASA AMES RESEARCH\n\nCENTER,\n\nMOFFETT\n\nFIELD,\n\nCALIFORNIA\n\nABSTRACT\n\nA computer code which solves the Navier-Stokes equations for three-dimensional,\ntime-dependent, homogeneous turbulence has been written for the Cyber 205. The\ncode has options fo both 64-hit and 32-hit arithmetic.\nWith 32-hit computation,\nmesh sizes up to 645 are contained within core of a 2 million 64-hit word memory.\nComputer speed timing runs were made for various vector lengths up to 6144.\nWith this code, speeds a little over 100 Mflops have been achieved on a 2-pipe\nCyber 205. Several problems encountered in the coding are discussed.\n1.\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nTurbulent fluid motion is common to many branches of engineering and science.\nSince turbulence phenomena are highly nonlinear, they are not amenable to classiConsequently, turbulence predictions are generally\ncal analytical approaches.\nbased on semi-empirical\nmodels. Experiments which generate model information\nare expensive, but are needed because current models are not generally accurate\nenough for engineering purposes. Detailed simulations of turbulent flows can help\ncomplement laboratory data. Direct numerical simulations of turbulent flows are\nmore accurate than current semi-empirical\ncomputational methods and can be\nused to both generate physical understanding and to improve the models. In these\nsimulations, turbulent flows are directly computed from the Navier-Stokes equations.\nComputations of this type are necessarily three-dimensional\nand timedependent; they require a large number of grid points, and thus, long computation\ntime.\nThe Cyber 205 computer appears ideally suited for efficient numerical\nsimulations of this type.\nExploration of the use of the Cyber 205 for direct\nnumerical simulation of turbulence is a principal objective of this work.\nThe basic code was written by one of the authors (RSR). It was modified to take\nadvantage of the 205 compiler\xe2\x80\x99s automatic vectorizing capability.\nVector syntax\nand special functions were applied to the code segments which could not be autoFinally, machine language instructions were used for the\nmatically vectorized.\nparts of the code that existing compiler could not handle.\n\n227\n\nIn the next section, a description of the particular problem to be solved is given.\nIn Section 3, the numerical methods used are discussed. This is followed by a brief\ndescription of the Cyber 205 at Colorado State University. The construction of\nlong vectors is discussed in Section 5. In Section 6, performance data obtained to\ndate are presented, and in Section 7, problems encountered are described. A typical simulation of homogeneous isotropic turbulence is presented in Section 8. In\nthe final section, a brief statement of conclusions is presented.\n2.\n\nPROBLEM\n\nSTATEMENT\n\nHomogeneous turbulent flows, of which there is a considerable variety, can be\nsimulated numerically at low Reynolds number without using any turbulence\nmodel. In the flows we will consider, the computational domain contains a fixed\nmass of fluid within a rectangular parallelepiped, the opposing sides of which can\nmove inward or outward with time. Thus, the cases which can be computed are\nquite varied: decaying homogeneous isotropic turbulence is generated if all six\nsides are stationary; turbulence undergoing uniform compression (or expansion) if\nall three pairs of sides move inward (outward) at same rate; turbulence undergoing\none-dimensional compression, if one pair of sides moves inward; or turbulence\nundergoing plane strain if one pair of sides moves inward at the same rate a\nsecond pair moves outward, while th.e. third pair remains stationary. Isotropic\nturbulence has been computed before, but turbulence undergoing compression or\nexpansion has not. The compression cases are of interest, for example, in internal\ncombustion engine modeling and in the interaction of turbulence with a shock\nwave.\nIt will be assumed that the Mach number is sufficiently small that the fluid is\ncompressed uniformly in space, so that the fluid density depends only on time.\nThe governing Navier-Stokes equations for a fluid of uniform viscosity and uniform density in space are:\n\nwhere ~1. P1, v 2, and t are fluctuating velocity components, fluctuating pressure,\nkinematii viscosity and time respectively. The summation convention is implied.\nThis set of governing Navier-Stokes equations allow us to simulate homogeneous\nturbulent flows in Lagrangian coordinate system that moves with the mean flow.\nCoordinate transformation tensor Bij is determined by:\n\nNote that mean strain rate tensor,%,\ngeneous turbulence.\n\nj is zero and Bij=sij\n228\n\nfor isotropic homo-\n\nPeriodic boundary conditions are applied in all three space directions.\nThe\nvelocity field is initialized to an isotropic state that satisfies continuity and has a\ngiven energy spectrum which approximates that of experimental isotropic turbulence.\n3.\n\nNUMERICAL\n\nMETHOD\n\nThe spectral method is used to compute all spatial derivatives.\nThis method,\nwhich uses FFT\xe2\x80\x99s, is good for problems with periodic boundary conditions and has\nvery high accuracy. To avoid aliasing ,in the nonlinear terms, both the truncation\nand phase shifting techniques are used.\nA second order Runge-Kutta method is used to advance the solution in time.\nThus, all spatial derivatives need to be computed twice each time step. The time\nstep was chosen small enough that no significant error is produced. It was determined by increasing the step size until the error was approximately 1 percent over\nthe full integration period.\n4.\n\nTHE CYBER 205\n\nThe Cyber 205 we are using is ty Colorado State machine with 2-pipes and a 2\nmillion 64-bit word fast memory.\nQTE Telenet has been used for data transfer\nbetween Stanford and CSU. We have found that both are reliable, convenient to\nuse, and have provided satisfactory service so far.\nFigure 1 shows the performance for add/multiply as function of vector length.\nThe asymptotic performance which requires maximum vector lengih (65535) is 100\nMflops for 64-hit arithmetic and 200 Mflops for 32-hit arithmetic.\nIt is obvious that the performance improves with vector length. Vector length\n1000 (64-hit case) or 2000 (32-bit case) is required to reach 90 percent of the\nasymptotic performance.\nConstructing a code which uses long vectors is therefore important if maximum performance from the machine is to be obtained.\n5.\n\nDATA MANAGEMENT\n\nBased on the \xe2\x80\x9clonger vector gives better performance\xe2\x80\x9d philosophy, we chose to do\nthe Fourier transforms in parallel. This will be explained in detail later.\nIn Figure 2, NX, NY, and NZ are the number of mesh points in the x, y, and z\ndirections respectively; MY and MZ are called \xe2\x80\x9cpencil sizes\xe2\x80\x9d.\nOn the first sweep, MZ x-y planes of data are Fourier transformed in the y direction in parallel.\nThe transform length is NY, but by doing them in parallel, a\nvector length of NX/2*MZ*3 is achieved; the factor 3 is due to the simultaneous\nprocessing of three velocity components, and the factor l/2 is due to only half of\nthe modes are needed in wave space to represent a real function in physical space.\nTo accomplish this, it is useful to lump every dependent variable into a single big\nThe main array in our code is DATA(NX/2,NY,NZ,4,2);\nthe dimensions\narray.\nrepresent x, y, z, a dependent variable index, and real and imaginary parts of a\ncomplex number.\n229\n\nc\n\n1oc\n\n2oc\n\n. . ,rrmr\n\nVECTOR\n\nFigure\n\nVECTOR\n\n. . I I , , ,,\n\n1\n\nL ENG TH\n\nRA TE\n\nI r,,#-f-m\n\nY MFLOP\n\nI I 1 I,,,,\n\nALILWMUL TIPL\nc\n\nL\n\nFigure\n\n2\n\nCOMPUTATIONAL\n\n-\n\n130X\n\nOn the second sweep, MY x-z planes are processed. Fourier transforms in z and x\nand\ndirections are done on this sweep. The vector lengths are NX/2*MY*3\nNZ*MY*3 respectively.\nA Cyber 205 vector\n\nis defined as a contiguous set of memory locations. Since the\ntwo sweeps are in different directions, an array transp:. ;t? has to be done between\nsweeps and within the second sweep in order to keep processed data in a\nThe transpose is done by using gather\ncontiguous set of memory locations.\nThe gather instruction puts array elements which are at various\ninstructions.\nlocations into a contiguous set of memory locations. An index vector is needed to\npick up desired elements. Q8VGATHR function (64-hit) or Q8VXTOV subroutine\n(32-hit) is used to do the transposing. As the array gets bigger, so does the index\nvector length, and an appreciable amount of overhead working space is needed. In\nthe 643 (32x16) run, the index vector has 17,408 elements.\n6.\n\nCOMPUTER\n\nPERFORMANCE\n\nThe performance data obtained to date, based on a hand count of the number of\noperations per time step, are presented in Table 1. The mesh size is given in\ncolumn 2 (each node requires 7 words of data storage). The pencil size is given in\ncolumn 3; this, together with mesh size, determines the vector length shown in\ncolumn 4. The computational precision is given in column 5, the CPU time in\ncolumn 6., and the CPU computation rate in column 7. The I/O time per step in\nseconds is meaningful only for runs with virtual memory paging. Explicit I/O\nwould reduce I/O time considerably, but we have not yet attempted to use explicit\nI/O.\nFigure 3 shows computation rate as function of vector length for our code on the\n2-pipe CSU Cyber 205. It approaches an asymptote as vector length increases.\nComparing Runs 3 and 4, and Runs 5 and 6 in Table 1, it is found that the CPU\ntime for a 32-hit (half) precision run is 60 percent of that for the corresponding\n64-hit (full) precision run. We kept track of the timing in the transpose part of\nthe code and found an interesting fact. In full precision runs, the transpose takes\n15 percent of the CPU time; 85 percent of the CPU time is spent in floating point\nIn half precision runs, due to the lack of a half precision gather\noperations.\nutility, the transpose takes the same amount of time as in full precision runs,\nwhile the floating point operations require only half of the full precision CPU\ntime. Consequently, for half precision run, the transpose takes 25 percent of the\ntotal time.\nDetailed timing from Run 8 shows that 51 percent of the CPU time is spent in the\nFFT subroutine, which contains 78 percent of the floating point operations.\nIn\nother words, the FFT operates at 157.6 Mflops. The remaining 22 percent of the\nfloating point operations are executed at 95 Mflops due mainly to shorter vector\nlengths and IF statements.\n7.\n\nPROBLEMS\n\nENCOUNTERED\n\nRuns 7 and 8 of Table 1 require 3.5M words storage, and hence, do not fit within\nthe 2M core memory at CSU with full precision. Thus, we must use 32-hit computation for efficient use of the CSU Cyber 205. Half-precision computation is\nsufficiently accurate for this code, and twice the operating speed is achieved.\n232\n\nh,\nW\nW\n\nI\n\n4x4\n\n8x8\n\n\\ NUlJI2.b\n\n32x32\n32x32\n16x16\n16x16\n32x16\n32x32\n\n32x32~32\n\n8X8X8\n\nI\n\n32x32~32\n\n32x32~32\n\n64x64~64\n\n64x6 4x64\n\n64x64~64\n\n64x64~64\n\nI\n\nI\n\n\\lN\n\n6,144\n\n4,608\n\n3,072\n\n3,072\n\n3,072\n\n3,072\n\n384\n\n192\n\nJ2P.L)\n\nI\n\n32\n\n32\n\n32\n\n64\n\n32\n\n64\n\n64\n\n64\n\nI\n\n59.6\n\n89.2\n\n53.6\n\n31.0\n\n23.5\n\n99.6\n\nI\n\n101.7\n105.2\n\n2.022\n\n3.378\n\n0.240\n\n0.399\n\n0.690\n\n0.014\n\n\\bLL.\n\nI\n\n1.980\n1.914\n\nI\n\nTABLE l.--PERFORMANCE OF CYBER 205 AT CSU\n(2 PIPES WITH 2M 64-BIT WORD)\n\n8.7\n\n-\n\n-\n\n56.6\n\n-\n\n-\n\n\\bLL.)\n\n3.52\n\n3.47\n\n2.70\n\n2.70\n\n0.69\n\n0.69\n\n0.30\n\n0.02\n\nMEMORY\nZP (M woRDs)\n\nIE\n\npaging\n\nin core\n\nin core\n\npaging\n\nin core\n\nin core\n\nin core\n\nin core\n\nCOMMENTS\n\nc\n\n1OC\n\n200\n\nFigure\n\n3\n\nVEC TOR LENGTH\n\n-I\n\n32\n\nBit\n\ncalls,\nSince there is no compiler avaipble -yet for half precision gather/scatter4\nwe have to use special Q8 calls (machine instructions) to get the half precision\ncode to compile properly on the CSU Cyber 205; the special Q8 instructions execute at full precision speed. Mr. Herbert Rothmund of CDC Sunnyvale was most\nhelpful to us in providing these utilities.\nIt is apparent that the I/O rate is not balanced with the CPU time. The reason is\nthat the CSU Cyber 205 has only two channels to transfer data between fast\nmemory and disk and they are inherently slow. Solid-state backing memory (or\nequivalent) would speed u% the data transfer rate. For our problem, faster I/O\nwould allow us to go to 128 mesh size.\nSince December 1982, three different compilers have been used: cycles 201109,\nL575, and 575B. Cycle 20 1109 did not have the half precision feature.\nCycle\nL575 had half precision but lacked some automatic vectorization features. Cycle\n575B, the most recent version, does not have gather/scatter\nin half precision.\nFurther improvements are needed if users are to get optimum performance from\nthis machine.\n8.\n\nSIMULATION\n\nOF ISOTROPIC\n\nHOMOGENEOUS\n\nTURBULENCE\n\nA typical simulation of homogeneous isotropic turbulent flow is presented in this\nsection. Figure 4 shows the time history of the three-dimensional\nenergy spectrum from initial time step to 300 time steps. Figure 5 shows the 3-D spectra of\nthe components of the turbulent kinetic energy at time step 300. The flow is\nThis is due to the small number of\nslightly anisotropic at low wavenumbers.\nmodes at low wavenumbers.\nAll of these resul +s are in excellent agreement with both experiments\xe2\x80\x99 and previous simulations.\nThus, we are confident that the code is performing satisfactorily and we will proceed to the simulation 9f compressed flows. The code\npresently runs at 1.9 second per time step for a 64 mesh on the 2-pipe Cyber 205;\nthis compares with 5 seconds for the same type of code on the CRAY-1S in\nVECTO RAL language.\n9.\n\nCONCLUSION\n\nIn summary, we have written, debugged, and tested a code for solving the NavierStokes equations and for computing various turbulence statistical quantities. Mos,J\nof the operations are readily vectorized, and 100 Mflops has been obtained for 64\nmesh size in-core runs on a 2-pipe Cyber 205. The major problems encountered so\nfar are concerned with the lack of compiler utilities, such as half-precision compiling capability for transpose operations.\nThe program works well and has been validated for homogeneous isotropic turbulence. The code will next be used to help develop turbulence models for compressed flow in engines.\n\n235\n\nIU\n\nVII\n\n10\xc2\xb0\n\n1\n\nI\n\n3-D\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\na\n\nI\n\nI\n\nFigure\n\n4\n\n10\xe2\x80\x99\nWAVE NO.\n\nI\n\n1\n\nENERGY SPEC TRUI\nI\nI\nI I I,..\n\n1\n\nI\n\n-2\n\n:\n\n1 o-4-\n\n1 C3:\n\n10\n\nFigure\n\n5\n\nWAVE NO.\n\nACKNOWLEDGMENTS\nThis work was supported by Control Data Corp. under project No. 828302. computer\nand a vector processor is the ability\nof the latter\nto produce a whole\narray ("vector")\nof results upon issuing a single hardware instruction.\nThe input to such a vector-instruction\nmay be one or two vectors,\none or\nor a combination of the above. The instructions\ntwo elements ("scalarsn),\nfall into two main categoriesthose that perform floating-point\narithmetic\n(including\nsquare root, sum, dot-product,\netc., as well as the basic\noperations),\nand those which may be collectively\ncalled "data-motion"\ninstructions.\nThese may be used,\nfor example, to "gather" elements from\none array into another using an arbitrary\n"index-list";\nto "compress" or\n"expand" an array; to "merge" two arrays into one (with arbitrary\nWiaterleaving"\npatterns),\netc.\n\none\n\nThe need for vector data-motion instructions\nbecomes apparent when\nconsiders the definition\nof a vector on a CDC CYBER 205. A vector is a set\n(array) of elements occupying consecutive\nlocations\nin memory. It means,\nby the way, that a vector may be represented in FORT&W by a multidimensional array; i.e;,\na two- or three-dimensional\narray may be used in\ncomputations as a single vector.\nThe reason for this vector definition\nis\nthat vhen performing vector operations on the CDC CYRER 205 the input\nelements are streamed directly\nfrom memory to the vector pipes and the\noutput is streamed directly\nback into memory without any intermediate\nregisters.\nThe timing formula for completing a vector\ninstruction\ncontains two\ncomponents. Oae is fixed, i.e.,\nindependent of the number of elements to\nbe computed, and is called "start-up"\ntime.\nIn fact, it amounts to\nstart-up and shut-down; it involves fetching\nthe pointers\nto the input and\noutput streams, aligning\nthe arrays so as to eliminate\nbask conflicts\nand\ngetting the first\npair of operands to the functional\nunit (the pipe-line)\nTypical time for the "start-up"\ncomponent\nand the last one back to memory.\nis 1 microsecond, or about 50 cycles (clock periods).\nThe other component\nof the timing formula is the l\'stream-time"\nwhich is proporatioaal\nto the\nnumber of elements in the vector.\nThe result rate for a Z-pipe CDC CYRER\n205 for an add or multiply\nis 2 results per cycle.\nIt is apparent now that\nin order to offset\nthe "wasted" cycles of starttimes it is beneficial\nto work with longer vectors.\nThe system is better utilized\nif a single\noperation is performed on a long vector,\nrather thaa several operations\nto\ncompute the same number\nof results.\nGiven a vector length, N, one can\nevaluate the efficiency\nof the computation\nas the ratio between the number\nof cycles used to compute results and the total number of cycles the\ninstruction\nhas taken; i.e.,\n(N/2)/(N/Z\n+ 50).\nThe maximum vector length\n300\n\nthe CDC CYBER 205 hardware allows is 65,535 elements.\nbecomes quite negligible\nlong before that.\n\nThe start-up\n\ntime\n\nare inserted through a\nThe vector "argumeats" for vector instructions\nIt is a quantity occupying 64 bits which\ncoastruct\ncalled it demands careful data structuring\nand analysis of the\nprocessing,\n"mapping" between the data and the operations\nto be performed, if the\nvector capabilities\nof the processor are to be efficiently\nutilized.\nWe\noperations-count\nas a measure\nhave also demonstrated that the traditional\nof processing time is not sufficient.\nOn a vector processor one has to\ntake into account\nthe\nnumber\nof vector operations\n(or\nthe lengths of the\nvectors) and the data-motion operations\n(which occur on a serial processor,\ntoo, but are often ignored when algorithms\nare evaluated).\nThe result of\nthe above is that one may have to re-examine the various parameters of the\nalgori4hm vhen migrating\nthe Multigrid\napplication\nfrom a serial to a\nvector processor.\nThis aspect requires further\ninvestigation.\n310\n\nVe feel\nthat\nthe experiment with the model-case studied in this paper\nwas successful and the performance achieved very pleasing.\nIt\ncertainly\nvarrants continuation\nuork.\nSome obvious areas we intend\nto engage in are\nthe following:\nExtending the application\nto three-dimensional\nPoisson\ncqua tioas\n; code a similar\napplication\nto cater for the, more general,\nDiffusion\nequation; and implement "full-weighting"\nresidual calculation\nand\ncubic interpolation.\nIn addition one may, of course, generalize\nthis uopk\nLn many directions.\nt\xc2\xb6ore general boundary conditions\n(Nemarm,\netc.) can\nbe implemented.\nThe solucioa of non-linear\nproblem8 (using PA8 multigrid\nversion) and systems of equations can also be vectorlted\nin a similar\nfashion.\nYore difficult,\nbut potentially\nimportant,\nis the exteneioo to\ngeneral drnnains, which vfll\nrequire a lot of thought about data structures\nand data motion.\nAs a last comment, it vlll\nk noted that all the timings\nquoted here were achieved using 64-bit arithmetic.\nOn the CDC CYBER 205\none can we 32-bit arithmetic\nas veil,\nand, thus, double the result rate\nfor vector\noperations Mile halving the memory requirements.\nFor the\nsmeller than truncation\nerrors in\npurpose of obtaining albebraic errors\nsolving second order equations,\nthe 32-bit arithmetic\nis indeed enough. We\nintend to examdne this option.\n\n[I.1\n\nA. Baadt,\nproblems",\n\n12.1\n\nV. Heckbusch and U. PLUNKETT,\nJOSEPH M. CYCHOSZ\nAND\nMICHAEL J. BAILEY\nPURDUE UNIVERSITY\nWEST LAFAYETTE,\n\nCADLAB\nINDIANA\n\nTEE\n\nVECTORIZATION\nFOR\n\nOF A RAY TRACING\nIMAGE\nGENERATION\n\nPROGRAM\n\nDavid J. Plunkett\xe2\x80\x99\nJoseph M. Cychosz\nMichael J. Bailey\n\nPurdue University\n\nCADLAB\n\nABSTRACT\nRay tracing is a widely used method for producing realistic computer-generated\nimages.\nRay tracing involves firing an imaginary ray from a view point, through a point on an image\nplane, into a three dimensional scene. The intersection of the ray with the objects in the scene\ndetermines what is visible at that point on the image plane. This process must be repeated\nmany times, once for each point (commonly called a pixel) in the image plane. A typical image\ncontains more than a million pixels making this process computationally\nexpensive. A traditional ray tracing program processes one ray at a time. In such a serial approach, as much as\nninety percent of the execution time is spent computing the intersection of a ray with the surfaces in the scene. With the CYBER 205, many rays can be intersected with all the bodies in\nVectorization\nof this intersection process\nthe scene with a single series of vector operations.\nresults in large decremes in computation\ntime.\nThe CADLAB\xe2\x80\x99s\ninterest in ray tracing stems from the need to produce realistic images of\nmechanical parts. A high quality image of a part during the design process can increase the\nproductivity\nof the designer by helping him visualize the results of his work. Abstract\n\nThis document\ncontains\nthe proceedings\nof the CYBER 200\nhosted\nby Goddard\nSpace Flight\nCenter\nApplications\nSeminar,\nwith\nControl\nData Corporation,\nheld on October\n10-12,\n1983.\nThe seminar\nwas attended\nby more than 100 participants,\nincluding\nrepresentatives\nfrom the United\nKingdom Meteorological\nOffice\nand CSIRO in Australia,\nfour other\ncountries,\nfifteen\nU.S. universities,\nten Federal\nresearch\ncenters,\nand\nnumerous\nprivate\nindustries.\nThe subjects\ncovered\nincluded\napplication\ntopics\nin Meteorology/Oceanography,\nChemistry,\nMath Algorithms,\nFluid\nDynamics,\nMonte Carlo\nMethods,\nElectronic\nCircuit\nSimulation,\nBiochemistry,\nPetroleum,\nLattice\nGuage Theory,\nEconomics,\nand Ray Tracing.\nThis\ndocument\nis comprised\nof the majority\nof the papers\npresented\nat the seminar.\n\n;7.
